    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4067665831407507835">The true story of an individual's struggle for self-identity, self-preservation, and freedom, <i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> remains among the few extant slave narratives written by a woman. This autobiographical account chronicles the remarkable odyssey of Harriet Jacobs (1813–1897) whose dauntless spirit and faith carried her from a life of servitude and degradati</span>
  <span id="freeText4067665831407507835" style="display:none">The true story of an individual's struggle for self-identity, self-preservation, and freedom, <i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> remains among the few extant slave narratives written by a woman. This autobiographical account chronicles the remarkable odyssey of Harriet Jacobs (1813–1897) whose dauntless spirit and faith carried her from a life of servitude and degradation in North Carolina to liberty and reunion with her children in the North.<br /><br />Written and published in 1861 after Jacobs' harrowing escape from a vile and predatory master, the memoir delivers a powerful and unflinching portrayal of the abuses and hypocrisy of the master-slave relationship. Jacobs writes frankly of the horrors she suffered as a slave, her eventual escape after several unsuccessful attempts, and her seven years in self-imposed exile, hiding in a coffin-like "garret" attached to her grandmother's porch.<br /><br />A rare firsthand account of a courageous woman's determination and endurance, this inspirational story also represents a valuable historical record of the continuing battle for freedom and the preservation of family.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4067665831407507835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1067312-in-the-chapter-titled-xvii-the-flight-i">In the chapter titled &quot;XVII. The Flight&quot; I could not wrap my mind around the idea of leaving ones children behind to hide in the attic for seven years. It is difficult to imagine the choices Harriet had to make throughout her life. For instance, she had an affair with Mr. Sands and had his children in hopes that it would get her away from Dr. Flint. What would you have done?</a>
</div>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I would probably have done the same. She really had no choice. She would be found otherwise. She needed to know and be connected to her children. If s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I would probably have done the same. She really had no choice. She would be found otherwise. She needed to know and be connected to her children. If she would have exposed herself, she would have been severely punished, and her children sold off.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1299636-did-anyone-think-that-the-writing-and">Did anyone think that the writing and vocabulary were quite advanced for someone who had no formal education?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1299636'><a id="like_count_community_question_1299636" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1299636?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F152519.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1299636-did-anyone-think-that-the-writing-and">2 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1299636-did-anyone-think-that-the-writing-and">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/684405-ann-michael"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1442480472i/684405._UY100_CR1,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/684405-ann-michael">Ann Michael</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">No. Compared with the formal prose of the era, her writing seems "simple"...not flowery and educated with metaphor and lengthy vocabulary. People wrot<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">No. Compared with the formal prose of the era, her writing seems "simple"...not flowery and educated with metaphor and lengthy vocabulary. People wrote and spoke differently in the 1830s than they do today, and remember that she was essentially a house slave and heard the white people speaking--and that she would have read the books that educated whites kept, the Bible and Ovid and such. <br /><br />Her editor says that she did not change the author's language, but more her chronology and structure of the memoir. This I can believe, too.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_520600294" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/520600294" />
    <a title="Petra-X" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x"><img alt="Petra-X" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603179398p2/1237196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/520600294?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra-X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra-X" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=biography-true-story">biography-true-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=caribbean">caribbean</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=popculture-anthropology">popculture-anthropology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=2016-150-reviews">2016-150-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer520600294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14492261271611856601">Harriet Jacobs book is quite a nuanced account of slavery from the point of view of one who is not physically abused. This does not make slavery any better, being owned and used and having no free will cannot ever be anything but terrible, but it was less painful. <br/><br/>For most slave owners slaves were extremely expensive farm animals and only the richest who could afford 'herds' of them would be able to maltreat them on a continual basis. If you want hard work from your oxen, and you want to breed f</span>
  <span id="freeText14492261271611856601" style="display:none">Harriet Jacobs book is quite a nuanced account of slavery from the point of view of one who is not physically abused. This does not make slavery any better, being owned and used and having no free will cannot ever be anything but terrible, but it was less painful. <br /><br />For most slave owners slaves were extremely expensive farm animals and only the richest who could afford 'herds' of them would be able to maltreat them on a continual basis. If you want hard work from your oxen, and you want to breed from your cows, they have to be kept healthy and in good condition. Well fed, rested, and with down-time. Not a life of ease or quality, not one without the whip, but one designed that the animals will do their job dawn to dusk and breed on a regular basis. So it was with slaves.<br /><br />However there is a line in a book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2367704.Caribbean_Slave_Society_and_Economy_A_Student_Reader" title="Caribbean Slave Society and Economy A Student Reader by Hilary Beckles" rel="nofollow noopener">Caribbean Slave Society and Economy: A Student Reader</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/172317.Hilary_Beckles" title="Hilary Beckles" rel="nofollow noopener">Beckles</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3038743.Verene_Shepherd" title="Verene Shepherd" rel="nofollow noopener">Shepherd</a> that says, "Within one year of the free market being established in Kingston, it was run by slaves much to everyone's satisfaction."<br /><br />What does that say? It says quite a few things. It says that the slaves had time and plots of land big enough to grow produce more than sufficient for their needs and of a high quality. It says that (some) slave masters allowed slaves time to go to market and sell their produce once a week which is allowing entrepreneurship. It says that the slaves were well-organised and commercially savvy. It says they had good customer service skills. <br /><br />What it says most of all was that Slaves were all victimised but not all became victims. And that is why there are so many successful Black islands in the Caribbean. But this is not to blame those were victims. It must have been very hard to have the strength of mind and character not to be when one is owned, beaten and treated far worse than the family dog. <br /><br />The best book I could recommend on slaves not being victims is Marlon James' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4682558.The_Book_of_Night_Women" title="The Book of Night Women by Marlon James" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Night Women</a>. A very enjoyable and instructive book that will have you cheering and rooting for some characters that do some very evil things. You might have to listen to it rather than read it though as it is almost all in Jamaican dialect.<br /><br />Read Jan 2013, reviewed Aug 2016.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14492261271611856601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_844874049" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/844874049" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/844874049?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6238735-incidents-in-the-life-of-a-slave-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=afro-american">afro-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=vintage">vintage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=slave-narratives">slave-narratives</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=women-and-books">women-and-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer844874049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13858997404556211779">This book was first published in 1861 and reprinted in the 1970s. Scholars initially doubted it was written by a slave. Thankfully, Harvard University Press authenticated and published findings of the 1980s, and Jean Fagan Yellin, Harriet Jacobs' biographer, dug up proof of the authenticity of this autobiography through letters and documents. I only regret not having the 1987 Harvard University Press edition edited by Yellin. <br/><br/>Jacobs seemed to anticipate the doubting Thomas, even as she wrote:<br/><blockquote>
  </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText13858997404556211779" style="display:none">This book was first published in 1861 and reprinted in the 1970s. Scholars initially doubted it was written by a slave. Thankfully, Harvard University Press authenticated and published findings of the 1980s, and Jean Fagan Yellin, Harriet Jacobs' biographer, dug up proof of the authenticity of this autobiography through letters and documents. I only regret not having the 1987 Harvard University Press edition edited by Yellin. <br /><br />Jacobs seemed to anticipate the doubting Thomas, even as she wrote:<br /><blockquote>
  <i> I hardly expect that the reader will credit me, when I affirm that I lived in that little dismal hole, almost deprived of light and air, and with no space to move my limbs, for nearly seven years. But it is a fact; and to me a sad one, even now; for my body still suffers from the effects of that long imprisonment, to say nothing of my soul. Members of my family, now living in New York and Boston, can testify to the truth of what I say.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Why the disbelief?Jacobs wrote under the pseudonym: Linda Brent, changing the names of the abolitionists and slave owners who had helped her. Legitimate reason for doubt. Jacobs' reason for changing the names, also understandable. <br /><br />Here's where it gets preposterous: Jacobs' prose was being compared to the male slave narratives. Instead of being in chronological order (hooray for the avid readers of contemporary creative nonfiction who find this cliche), hers was told according to vivid incidents in her life. Hint: the title. In addition, she seemed like the heroine of a romance novel, scholars said. It was just so unfathomable, that this woman, this slave, could have been chased in such a manner, by an obsessive slave master whose wife mistreated her because she was so insanely jealous of her. Why hide in such a place that resembled a coffin, for so many years, just because your master wanted you as his concubine? It all seemed unbelievable. <br /><br />Yet it wasn't. <br /><br />Jacobs' life was different than most. She was raised by a kind slave owner who educated her, gave her grandmother her freedom, and yet died before Harriet could get her freedom. She was of mixed race and had a father who also died before buying her freedom. She was never beaten, never saw hard labor, and raised with a keen understanding of the world: <i> I was never cruelly overworked; I was never lacerated with the whip from head to foot…I never had my heel-strings cut to prevent my running away; I was never chained to a log and forced to drag it about, while I toiled in the fields…"</i><br /><br />When she ran away, this was the posting made by her slave owner:<br /><blockquote> An intelligent, bright, mulatto girl…dark eyes, and black hair inclined to curl; but it can be made straight. Has a decayed spot on a front tooth. She can read and write. </blockquote><br /><br />This is the second time I've read this account, but the first time I've captured it in its entirety. Slavery is something that never ceases to baffle me. How could my ancestors have been treated so cruelly, like mere animals, yet trusted with the food and babies of their "owners?" How could they have been viewed "unfit" as humans, yet fit enough to breastfeed their "masters' " infants? Reading this, I paused to consider the many black mothers who raised white families, because when you really consider the intimacy of breastfeeding, you know that black slave mothers were giving white babies the same nutrients from their body that they gave their black babies. They weren't good enough to eat from their "masters' tables, yet good enough to stick a nipple in their "masters'"mouths. The hypocrisy and irony. Speaking of intimacy, think of the act of someone leaving his "slave's" sex bed and entering his wife's sex bed. In the end, women as a unit, became the victimized.<br /><br />This is what Jacobs seems to imply here, with her themes of women as sex objects, and women as slaves who treated each other as slaves; the black woman and the victimized white woman as her "master." Most times you only hear about the crazed sexual acts but in this book, you see that at times, slave owners were in love with, and obsessed with their female slaves, even sometimes arranging for them to occupy the vacation homes away from the wives. What Jacobs does in this narrative is speak directly to the issues of women during slavery, (the wife, lover, and child) something that had not been done in previous narratives. <br /><br />This narrative also highlighted something important for me: The Fugitive Slave Act. Imagine a life of always being on the run from the law, just because you were demanding your freedom. Previously, slaves could always escape to the North and find refuge. With this act, their southern slave owners could go up north and seize them while they walked to church with their family. What an emotional roller coaster:<br /><blockquote>
  <i> Many a wife discovered a secret she had never known before--that her husband was a fugitive and must leave her to insure his own safety. Worse still, many a husband discovered that his wife had fled from slavery years ago, and as "the child follows the condition of its mother," the children of his love were liable to be seized and carried into slavery.</i>
</blockquote> I learned about the Fugitive Act in history classes but never truly grasped the meaning of it until reading this book. I'm just glad that for Black History Month, I could revisit this.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13858997404556211779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3051395191" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3051395191" />
    <a title="Dem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2752994-dem"><img alt="Dem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552868566p2/2752994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3051395191?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dem" href="/user/show/2752994-dem">Dem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19467277-incidents-in-the-life-of-a-slave-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2752994-dem?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3051395191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8429170077262874013"><b>A remarkable and vivid autobiography that details the life of Harriet Jacobs as a slave in North Carolina in the mid 1800s.</b><br/><br/><i>
  <b>My Master had power and law on his side. I had a determined will. There is might in each.</b>
</i> Quote from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152519.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl' title='Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl by Harriet Ann Jacobs' rel='nofollow noopener'>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</a><br/><br/>This should be required reading in YA and history students in schools as it is documents the author’s life as a slave and her fight for freedom for herself and her children. An account in which female slaves are subjected to sexual abu</span>
  <span id="freeText8429170077262874013" style="display:none"><b>A remarkable and vivid autobiography that details the life of Harriet Jacobs as a slave in North Carolina in the mid 1800s.</b><br /><br /><i>
  <b>My Master had power and law on his side. I had a determined will. There is might in each.</b>
</i> Quote from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/152519.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl" title="Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl by Harriet Ann Jacobs" rel="nofollow noopener">Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</a><br /><br />This should be required reading in YA and history students in schools as it is documents the author’s life as a slave and her fight for freedom for herself and her children. An account in which female slaves are subjected to sexual abuse, the sale of their siblings and children, a life of torture, mental and physical cruelty with little hope of freedom, or even inner peace. <br />These types of books make me angry and frustrated and certainly don't make for pleasant reading and yet we NEED to read these books to inform, and educate us and most of all so we never forget the pain and injustice that was inflicted upon our fellow humans in the not so distant past. <br /><br />I happened to listen to this one on Audible and the narration was quite poor and I really would not recommend it as a book of this importance deserves a narrator that can tell the story with clarity and without a forced southern drawl.<br /><br />I am so glad I came across this book as I have read quite a few historical fiction novels on slavery but this was an eye opener and certainly a book I will remember a long time from now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8429170077262874013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1571028153" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1571028153" />
    <a title="Samadrita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita"><img alt="Samadrita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461258628p2/13266158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1571028153?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samadrita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samadrita" href="/user/show/13266158-samadrita">Samadrita</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<div id="review_2027536661" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2027536661" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7365405-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522094190p2/7365405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2027536661?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/7365405-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=1-fiction">1-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=4-written-pre-20th-century">4-written-pre-20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2027536661" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer208638942805773810"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br/> Harriet Ann Jacob’s work was similar to Frederick Douglass’ narrative in that both of the pieces read so quickly and easily. I very much enjoyed Jacob’s piece. The language seemed so real and almost as though Harriet, or Linda, was telling the story to me herself. I understood the work very easily also probably because I had previously read Douglass’ piece which showed the life of a slave who was beaten viciously at times. Jacobs, who experienced a very different type of </span>
  <span id="freeText208638942805773810" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br /> Harriet Ann Jacob’s work was similar to Frederick Douglass’ narrative in that both of the pieces read so quickly and easily. I very much enjoyed Jacob’s piece. The language seemed so real and almost as though Harriet, or Linda, was telling the story to me herself. I understood the work very easily also probably because I had previously read Douglass’ piece which showed the life of a slave who was beaten viciously at times. Jacobs, who experienced a very different type of slavery was more mentally abused than physically abused. She was a strong woman who I admired very much. I thought she made a few mistakes in her life, but she was a role model for all the other slaves.<br />	Jacob’s work has shown the awful side of life like Douglass had, but Jacobs story was aimed more towards a white women’s audience (from the Intro...). Either way, she has shown the struggle of a woman who wants to free her children, and so she is also fighting for herself. She wants to free herself from the burdens of Mr. Flint. Jacobs work definitely is a strong model for women who are fighting to free themselves from the wrongs of society. She is a good representative of, at the same time, a woman from the mid 19th century who s trying to escape. She may not have suffered them same persecutions as every other slave, but she still suffered.<br />	It flowed so smoothly and really gained an interest from the readers. It hit home for some people and for others it just tugged on their heart strings some more. I think that it is very important for people to read this piece of literature because it gives a representation of a different side or type of American life. It is a part of our culture (back then) and a part of our history. Overall, I really liked this work and would recommend it to anyone.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>About Me</u>
  </i>
</b><br />For those new to me or my reviews... here's the scoop: I read A LOT. I write A LOT. And now I blog A LOT. First the book review goes on Goodreads, and then I send it on over to my WordPress blog at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://thisismytruthnow.com">https://thisismytruthnow.com</a>, where you'll also find TV &amp; Film reviews, the revealing and introspective 365 Daily Challenge and lots of blogging about places I've visited all over the world. And you can find all my social media profiles to get the details on the who/what/when/where and my pictures. Leave a comment and let me know what you think. Vote in the poll and ratings. Thanks for stopping by.</span>

<div id="review_3419395351" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3419395351" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3536004-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535136437p2/3536004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3419395351?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/3536004-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=memoir-biography">memoir-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3536004-jenna?shelf=classic-of-the-month">classic-of-the-month</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3419395351" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8201200152631666465"><a title='Gilbert Studios, Washington, D.C.  (C. M. Gilbert); restored by Adam Cuerden / Public domain' href='https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Gilbert_Studios_photograph_of_Harriet_Jacobs.jpg' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img width='256' alt='Gilbert Studios photograph of Harriet Jacobs' src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1593781442i/29756610.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>Harriet Ann Jacobs</b><br/><br/><i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> is a harrowing account of one woman's journey from slavery to freedom. It is one of only a few remaining slave narratives written by and about women.<br/><br/>Harriet Ann Jacobs was born into slavery but always knew she deserved to be free. Her younger years were spent under a "kind" mistress (though what an oxymoron... a kind owner of another human being?). <br/><br/>After this mistress died, it appeared she wasn't so kind after all. Instead of setting Ha</span>
  <span id="freeText8201200152631666465" style="display:none"><a title="Gilbert Studios, Washington, D.C.  (C. M. Gilbert); restored by Adam Cuerden / Public domain" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Gilbert_Studios_photograph_of_Harriet_Jacobs.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img width="256" alt="Gilbert Studios photograph of Harriet Jacobs" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1593781442i/29756610.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Harriet Ann Jacobs</b><br><br><i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> is a harrowing account of one woman's journey from slavery to freedom. It is one of only a few remaining slave narratives written by and about women.<br><br>Harriet Ann Jacobs was born into slavery but always knew she deserved to be free. Her younger years were spent under a "kind" mistress (though what an oxymoron... a kind owner of another human being?). <br><br>After this mistress died, it appeared she wasn't so kind after all. Instead of setting Harriet (or Linda, her pseudonym in the book) and her brother free, she bequeathed them to others. Linda was given to her mistress' infant niece whose father was a lecherous and evil man who hounded Linda mercilessly. Like other slave owners, Flint saw her not as another human being but as a piece of property to be used however he wished. <br><br>Linda eventually had two children and when Dr. Flint threatened to send them to a plantation to "break them" if she did not succumb to his wishes, young Linda ran away rather than let him use her children as a pawn. She hid in a tiny crawl space under the roof of a shed in her grandmother's backyard.<br><br>With the current pandemic, it is helpful to put things into perspective. Many act like it is persecution to be asked to stay home as much as possible. In our own homes, with all our modern comforts. Safe.<br><br>Linda lived for <i>seven years</i> in a space she couldn't even stand up in. A space measuring 9 X 7 X 3 feet. 3 feet at its highest.  (For my non American friends: 2.7 X 2.1 X .91 meters.). Seven years in that tiny space, constantly in fear of being discovered. Chattel slaves who ran away were put through the most unimaginable horrors if they were caught. The fear Linda lived with every single day and hour and minute of her life would have destroyed a weaker person. <br><br>Thankfully this young woman eventually was able to flee to the North and, in spite of the Fugitive Slave Act which said Northerners had to return any escaped slaves to their Southern "owners", Linda was able to evade capture for years until a kind employer purchased her freedom.<br><br><i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> is Linda/Harriet's autobiography. She writes beautifully though harrowingly. It is never easy to read of the tortures endured by chattel slaves in America and it would have been extremely difficult for Harriet to pen these words, reliving the excruciating circumstances she endured all because of the color of her skin.<br><br>Harriet remained ever hopeful, in spite of witnessing unspeakable crimes against herself and her fellow slaves. Her spirit was indomitable. <br><br>The book reads almost like a novel. Unfortunately for millions, chattel slavery was not fiction. <br><br>Harriet wrote this book in hopes of exposing the horrors of slavery to Northerners, to win them over to the abolitionist cause. Her courage and fortitude are remarkable. She writes with deep insight of the mind of the slave and the mind of the slave owner.<br><br>This is a book I think all Americans should read. <b>We do not need statues honoring Confederate soldiers in order to remember history. We need to read books like this.</b><br><br>Highly recommend.<br><br>(<i>Photo on Wikimedia Commons. Attributions: Gilbert Studios, Washington, D.C. (C. M. Gilbert); restored by Adam Cuerden)<br><br>~July 2020 classic-of-the-month</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8201200152631666465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1221905614" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1221905614" />
    <a title="Nicole~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20071931-nicole"><img alt="Nicole~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439784920p2/20071931.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1221905614?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole~" href="/user/show/20071931-nicole">Nicole~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780674002715" href="/book/show/481945.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl_Written_by_Herself_Enlarged_Edition_Now_with_A_True_Tale_of_Slavery_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=autobiography">autobiography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=woman-writer">woman-writer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20071931-nicole?shelf=slavery">slavery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1221905614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8088003732416140187"><b>
  <i>Reader, it is not to awaken sympathy for myself that I am telling you truthfully what I suffered in slavery. I do it to kindle the flame of compassion in your heart for my sisters who are still in bondage, suffering as I once suffered.</i>
</b><br/><br/>In the pre-civil war period of 1861, Harriet Jacobs was the only black woman in the United States to have authored her own slave narrative, in a call to <i>"arouse the women of the North to a realizing sense of the condition of two millions of women at the South..</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8088003732416140187" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Reader, it is not to awaken sympathy for myself that I am telling you truthfully what I suffered in slavery. I do it to kindle the flame of compassion in your heart for my sisters who are still in bondage, suffering as I once suffered.</i>
</b><br><br>In the pre-civil war period of 1861, Harriet Jacobs was the only black woman in the United States to have authored her own slave narrative, in a call to <i>"arouse the women of the North to a realizing sense of the condition of two millions of women at the South...to convince the people of the Free States what slavery really is."</i> Jacobs hoped that, should the white women of the North know the true conditions of the slave women of the South, they would not fail to answer the call to moral action. With the help of a northern abolitionist, Jacobs published this astounding, poignant record under the pseudonym <i>Linda Brent.</i><br><br>She was a slave woman, who for seven years lived in a tiny attic space in her grandmother's house before making her escape to the north. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/481945.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl_Written_by_Herself_Enlarged_Edition_Now_with_A_True_Tale_of_Slavery_" rel="nofollow noopener">Incidents</a>, she recounts her story from her childhood, writing in lyrical and intimate tones which, in spite of its painful, agonizing rhetoric, coaxes the sensibilities of the reader. To <i>Linda's</i> credit, accounts of the ugly features in the daily life of a female slave; atrocities, treachery, humiliation; the lengths taken to evade the licentious abusers free at hand to mistreat, with impunity and the sanction of social (and religious) law; being hunted and separated from her children, were narrated with striking control and measured emotion, so that the reader is not overwhelmed by the lashes of such oppressive events.<br><br>As a black slave woman, <i>Linda</i> suffered hardships unimaginable to women of the North. She spent most of her adolescent life in the household of Dr. Flint, barely able to keep at bay his lecherous sexual advances. Flint was consumed by a neurotic obsession that grew in severity and viciousness whenever his sexual harassments were foiled. <i>"My master met me at every turn, reminding me that I belonged to him, and swearing by heaven and earth that he would compel me to submit to him."</i><br><br><i>Linda</i> could not accept the destruction of her moral and physical being, that is to say, by the conventional sexualizing of a female slave. She refused to become the passive female victim - instead, she was fiercely determined to protect her virtue and to steer its destiny herself, to uphold her dignity, to seek freedom or die in her attempt. As a final recourse to escape the grasp of one she hated and knowing it would outrage him, she succumbed instead to a kindly white neighbor to whom she would bear two children.<br><br> <b>
  <i>When he told me I was made for his use, made to obey his command in every thing; that I was nothing but a slave, whose will must and should surrender to his, never before had my puny arm felt half so strong... The war of my life had begun; and though one of God's most powerless creatures, I resolve never to be conquered.</i>
</b><br><br><i>Linda</i> writes of her struggle to protect her womanhood with an ironclad sense of self, a determination to maintain an autonomous identity impenetrable to assault. She contrasts the roles of the female slave juxtaposed with those of the white female slaveholder, as they both existed under the same 'patriarchal roof,' and offers that, as hateful as slave owners were, there were those who were good and benevolent, though not in equal portions. She puts in grave context the uniquely female burdens of slavery: how inhumanly debauched and grotesquely disfigured white slaveholders were, being empowered by ownership of female slaves.<br><br><b>
  <i>Women are considered of no value, unless they continually increase their owners stock. They are put on a par with animals. This same master shot a woman through the head who had run away and been brought back to him...The master who did these things was highly educated, and styled a perfect gentleman. He also boasted the name and standing of a Christian, though Satan never had a truer follower.</i>
</b><br><br>Landing on Philadelphia's soil, having severed the bonds from 'master,' was the ultimate triumph for the naturally virtuous spirit that could never acknowledge itself to be 'chattel.' <i>Linda</i> managed to keep the reins on her own destiny, pride and dignity; in her memory, she stored the love of family she left behind, and to her heart most dearly, she held her children. <br><br>An important historicization of the female slave role or a victorious feminist's literature, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/481945.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl_Written_by_Herself_Enlarged_Edition_Now_with_A_True_Tale_of_Slavery_" rel="nofollow noopener">Incidents</a> also exists as a testimonial of tragic human losses in an oppressive institution, and a solemn reminder of those who did not escape it. For Harriet Jacobs aka <i>Linda Brent</i> , it is both a blood-soaked lamentation and an enlightening melody of the break from the chains that bound her. She concludes with subtext as impressive as her escape strategy: <i>"Reader, my story ends with freedom; not in the usual way, with marriage."</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425832683i/13939565.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> Harriet Ann Jacobs, 1813-1897 <br> Fugitive Slave, Writer, Abolitionist</span>
  <a data-text-id="8088003732416140187" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15224603313649266454">Never having read a memoir written by a person living under the yoke of slavery, I found this autobiography painful and enlightening.   <span id="freeText15224603313649266454" style="display:none">Never having read a memoir written by a person living under the yoke of slavery, I found this autobiography painful and enlightening. Harriet Jacobs must have been a wonderfully strong woman to endure what she did and to demand her full rights as a human being. She refused to give in to the sexual demands of her owner. Let's examine that word a moment, her "owner" wanted to have sex with her, a teenage girl who already is working for the family and at their call 24 hours a day. Harriet could never get away from him. Supposedly, a doctor, this disgusting excuse for a man, chose to spend his middle age pursuing a young girl night and day.<br />She would rather give herself freely to another white man, a lawyer in his 20s who she thought might be able to help her.<br />Rather then submit to the doctor she eventually is hidden in a small attic space in the roof of her grandmother's house for SEVEN years. What tenacity!</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2027121466?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenda" href="/user/show/2337007-brenda">Brenda</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13504943-incidents-in-the-life-of-a-slave-girl-written-by-herself">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2337007-brenda?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2337007-brenda?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2337007-brenda?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2337007-brenda?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2337007-brenda?shelf=own-read">own-read</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2027121466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2978390815132032128">Filled with sadness, heartache and misery, <i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> is the personal story of Harriet Ann Jacobs, known as Linda. Linda was born into slavery and enjoyed a life of childish happiness for a short time. But when her mother’s new owner Dr Flint took control of the slaves, life changed for his unfortunate chattels. For he was a cruel and vindictive man, always free with the whip and chain for any slight misdemeanour. The majority of the slave holders were this way; it was</span>
  <span id="freeText2978390815132032128" style="display:none">Filled with sadness, heartache and misery, <i>Incidents in the Life of a Slave Girl</i> is the personal story of Harriet Ann Jacobs, known as Linda. Linda was born into slavery and enjoyed a life of childish happiness for a short time. But when her mother’s new owner Dr Flint took control of the slaves, life changed for his unfortunate chattels. For he was a cruel and vindictive man, always free with the whip and chain for any slight misdemeanour. The majority of the slave holders were this way; it was rare to find someone who was kind to their slaves…<br /><br />Slavery was part of life in the South in the 1800s - fine if you were a slave holder; a master – shocking if you were mulatto as Linda and her family were. This story was hard to read knowing it’s all true – all authenticated. It’s a well written account of the brutality of the era; an emotional and worthwhile read which I recommend.<br /><br />You’ll find this one free to download on Project Gutenberg.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2978390815132032128" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

I couldn't put it down - finished the whole thing within 30 hours. Slavery is such a heartbreaking thing - this book really helped me understand how devastating it was and why it had such a lasting impact on our society. Highly recommend. </span>

<div id="review_123271082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123271082" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2669250-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316968750p2/2669250.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123271082?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/2669250-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123271082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17997157733012404108">Letters of a Slave Girl by Mary Lyons was recommended to me, and maybe that one is easier to read than this book. That is a novel based on the life of Harriet Jacobs, and this book was actually written by her. She was a slave in the town I grew up in. It's been hard for me to finish it because it is really hard to let my mind be taken into a society like that. Her owner was a prominent member of the community, the doctor. I keep thinking, "I'm so glad I have never heard that the town doctor was </span>
  <span id="freeText17997157733012404108" style="display:none">Letters of a Slave Girl by Mary Lyons was recommended to me, and maybe that one is easier to read than this book. That is a novel based on the life of Harriet Jacobs, and this book was actually written by her. She was a slave in the town I grew up in. It's been hard for me to finish it because it is really hard to let my mind be taken into a society like that. Her owner was a prominent member of the community, the doctor. I keep thinking, "I'm so glad I have never heard that the town doctor was a part of my ancestry." But it has made me wonder what my ancestors at that time did think and accept about slavery. How did my forebears treat their slaves? I know they had them. What would I have thought and done if I was raised in that time? I have never heard of the sexual exploitation of the slave girls the way she portrays it in this book. It's so hard to believe that something like that was so prevalent and accepted. The brutality of that era is so awful to even think about. An interesting and emotional read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17997157733012404108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38964418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16114741881296698509">You know, for being such a short book, this one packs a wallop. I think that we're all used to stories about the brutality and horrors of slavery, and that is a part of this memoir as well, but mostly it is focused on how degrading and dehumanizing and mentally torturous it is to be considered someone's property, to be used and treated however they feel, as though you're a throw rug to be taken out and beat for a while. <br/><br/>I don't think that there's much that I could say about this book that hasn't</span>
  <span id="freeText16114741881296698509" style="display:none">You know, for being such a short book, this one packs a wallop. I think that we're all used to stories about the brutality and horrors of slavery, and that is a part of this memoir as well, but mostly it is focused on how degrading and dehumanizing and mentally torturous it is to be considered someone's property, to be used and treated however they feel, as though you're a throw rug to be taken out and beat for a while. <br /><br />I don't think that there's much that I could say about this book that hasn't already been said. Harriet Jacobs lived life at so many odds - she was a slave, but a well-treated one, by most standards. She wasn't beaten, or worked to the brink of death. She wasn't forced to watch her children be sold away from her one by one, as many other slave mothers were. She wasn't raped. In her community, her family was pretty well-respected and even loved, despite their being chattel. <br /><br />But the degradation and humiliation and constant risk of abuse and assault and death at the whim of someone who would be legally protected, and even socially justified, in mistreating you is just as punishing. So what do you choose? The horrific life you know and have come to expect, the life that is not your own, where your family could be snatched from you at a moment's notice... or do you risk everything and try to be free? <br /><br />Harriet chose the latter, and in doing so willingly constrained herself to a life much smaller and harder than she'd ever had to live through before - but with the prospect of freedom at the end of it.<br /><br />In a lot of ways, this book reminded me of Anne Frank's diary. The story of people so persecuted that their option is to be hauled away to be mistreated and killed, or to hide in cramped quarters with the hope of being overlooked and one day find their way to a better life. Harriet's story took place many years before Anne's did... but they both hurt my soul. <br /><br />I'm glad that things worked out for Harriet. I only wish more had been as lucky. Or, if I could have a real wish granted, I'd wish that humans were just better people in general, and that we didn't feel the need to subjugate, exploit, and kill others for their own gain. Just sayin'. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16114741881296698509" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2101688324" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2101688324" />
    <a title="Latanya (Crafty Scribbles)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles"><img alt="Latanya (Crafty Scribbles)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522811134p2/24192262.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2101688324?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Latanya (Crafty Scribbles)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Latanya (Crafty Scribbles)" href="/user/show/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles">Latanya (Crafty Scribbles)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/8503776-incidents-in-the-life-of-a-slave-girl-written-by-herself">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=food-for-the-melaninated-soul">food-for-the-melaninated-soul</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=society">society</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24192262-latanya-crafty-scribbles?shelf=women">women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2101688324" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12312830893990328087">(Courtesy of my blog, <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.craftyscribbles.com'>www.craftyscribbles.com</a>)<br/><br/>An unflinching account of Harriet Jacobs' life as woman "living" as a slave. I place living in quotations to demonstrate the difference between living a life, which connotes freedom, and surviving a life, which illustrates a resemblance of a life within another's desire to wrap cruel albatrosses around your neck, proverbial and literal.<br/><br/>Ms. Jacobs' life began as a slave. She's never known freedom otherwise until her heartbreaking story leads to her fr</span>
  <span id="freeText12312830893990328087" style="display:none">(Courtesy of my blog, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.craftyscribbles.com">www.craftyscribbles.com</a>)<br /><br />An unflinching account of Harriet Jacobs' life as woman "living" as a slave. I place living in quotations to demonstrate the difference between living a life, which connotes freedom, and surviving a life, which illustrates a resemblance of a life within another's desire to wrap cruel albatrosses around your neck, proverbial and literal.<br /><br />Ms. Jacobs' life began as a slave. She's never known freedom otherwise until her heartbreaking story leads to her freedom. Through various vignettes, she paints the picture of life with the evil industry, known as slavery. From favoritism based on complexion (i.e., fair-skinned slaves received less arduous mistreatment than darker toned counterparts) to daily sexual harassment to fear of losing one's children via kidnapping and the slave trade, she chronicles the indescribable ugliness of humans toward other humans. <br /><br />To call this story heartbreaking and unflinching feels basic. I could throw all the twenty-dollar words my education via blood, sweat, and tears of my ancestors sacrificed for me at this piece. Yet, I'd still feel unworthy of describing what she shares in the book, as if it's not my place to put her pain and the pain of others into words. I should just listen and learn without repeating the lack of humanity demonstrated. I shall not outline too much because I want you to capture the story yourself.<br /><br />But, indeed, inhumanity prevails as one clear comparison of inhumanity appears. To save herself from her owner's incessant sexual harassment, Ms. Jacobs hides in hutch in the attic, as did Anne Frank, hiding from Nazis in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48855.The_Diary_of_a_Young_Girl" title="The Diary of a Young Girl by Anne Frank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Diary of a Young Girl</a>. History insists on repeating...and we insist on ignoring the signs.<br /><br />Unfortunately, when slavery's discussed, we focus on male slaves. Their stories ring truer, due to misogyny and sexism. Female slaves and their stories find themselves in the back. Perhaps, their stories present harsher tales, including rape, sexual harassment, and watching their children kidnap and sold miles from them at any given age. Their pain rings deeper and many wish to not surround themselves in the deeper and complex horrors they offer, which causes further pain, as their stories require discussion and recognition too.<br /><br />Just like Frederick Douglass' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36529.Narrative_of_the_Life_of_Frederick_Douglass" title="Narrative of the Life of Frederick Douglass by Frederick Douglass" rel="nofollow noopener">Narrative of the Life of Frederick Douglass</a>, this story, along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/571420.Jubilee" title="Jubilee by Margaret Walker" rel="nofollow noopener">Jubilee</a>, deserve study in America's classrooms without delay. Their pain is our pain, and we should never forget them.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>Verdict</u>
</b>: Highly recommended. 5/5 Classroom lessons</span>
  <a data-text-id="12312830893990328087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1442426086" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1442426086" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1442426086?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451527523" href="/book/show/666842.Incidents_in_the_Life_of_a_Slave_Girl">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">This is THE autobiography of slavery</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=non-fiction-stuff">non-fiction-stuff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1442426086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5063872482348149196">"<i>READER be assured this narrative is no fiction. I am aware that some of my adventures may seem incredible but they are nevertheless strictly true. I have not exaggerated the wrongs inflicted by Slavery, on the contrary, my descriptions fall far short of the facts. I have concealed the names of places and given persons fictitious names. I had no motive for secrecy on my own account, but I deemed it kind and considerate towards others to pursue this course. <br/><br/>I wish I were more competent to the tas</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5063872482348149196" style="display:none">"<i>READER be assured this narrative is no fiction. I am aware that some of my adventures may seem incredible but they are nevertheless strictly true. I have not exaggerated the wrongs inflicted by Slavery, on the contrary, my descriptions fall far short of the facts. I have concealed the names of places and given persons fictitious names. I had no motive for secrecy on my own account, but I deemed it kind and considerate towards others to pursue this course. <br /><br />I wish I were more competent to the task I have undertaken. But I trust my readers will excuse deficiencies in consideration of circumstances. I was born and reared in Slavery and I remained in a Slave State twenty seven years. Since I have been at the North it has been necessary for me to work diligently for my own support and the education of my children. This has not left me much leisure to make up for the loss of early opportunities to improve myself and it has compelled me to write these pages at irregular intervals whenever I could snatch an hour from household duties.</i>" - From preface. <br /><br /><br />I have been waiting for awhile to write about this book. I have read and listened to a few slave narratives, but I believe, though the narration is very 19th century, the content is the most compelling slave narrative ever written. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18943.Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass" rel="nofollow noopener">Frederick Douglass</a>' autobiography is the most well-known and most eloquent, but this story by Harriet Jacobs is the most incredible and powerful and offers a view of slavery that many people do not really read about. We get to see slavery through the eyes, not only of a "house slave", but of a woman who was enslaved. Like Douglass, Jacobs was mixed-race and secretly became literate. But unlike him, she had the use of pure physical violence (sort-of) replaced with constant sexual violence (since age 12). Her time in slavery was between being molested and harassed by her master and being beaten and abused by her master's wife out of jealousy. One of the ways she initially tries to stop this is by taking a white lover who she had two children by him. This...did not work and when her likelihood of being out-right raped and her children being sold "down the river" became more likely, she pulled off the most amazing escape from slavery I have ever read.<br /><br />I do not want to reveal too much, but if you are familiar with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1813878.The_Diary_of_a_Young_Girl" title="The Diary of a Young Girl by Anne Frank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Diary of a Young Girl</a> this is where we are going. Jacobs had twice tried to escape, but was caught so she pulls off an amazing gambit on her third try. While some slaves escaped, they did not always go straight north. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Jacobs hid in a space in her grandmother's house only a little bigger than the hole that Saddam Hussein was found in for seven years and finally makes her way to New York. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> She tries to just work for an abolitionist while getting her children out of slavery (which she does) and was almost captured, which prompts for her to finally be legally purchased and freed by her abolitionist sponsor. <br /><br />This book, beyond its story of Harriet/"Linda", adds a powerful feminist critique to slavery that is missing from other slave narratives of the time. The suffering of women as daughters, sisters, and mothers (and grandmothers) is examined in a personal way that you do not get with a Frederick Douglass. What is interesting also is that she talks in the beginning of how she did not realize that she was a slave until she was six years old. It reminds of when I reflected on my own childhood and realized that I did not know I lived in a ghetto until I was I was around seven; I am not a expert of child psychology, but that is still worth note. We know now of the horrors that African-American women faced during slavery, but this was a revolutionary account in 1861 when this book was published. Harriet Jacobs was a busy woman and she was as dedicated to the cause of freedom and respect for African-Americans as anyone during the 19th century. Her brother also published a slave narrative.<br /><br />"<i>Reader my story ends with freedom; not in the usual way, with marriage. I and my children are now free! I We are as free from the power of slaveholders as are the white people of the north; and though that, according to my ideas, is not saying a great deal, it is a vast improvement in <u>my</u> condition. The dream of my life is not yet realized. I do not sit with my children in a home of my own. I still long for a hearthstone of my own, however humble. I wish it for my children's sake far more than for my own. But God so orders circumstances as to keep me with my friend Mrs. Bruce. Love, duty, gratitude, also bind me to her side. It is a privilege to serve her who pities my oppressed people, and who has bestowed the inestimable boon of freedom on me and my children. <br /><br />It has been painful to me, in many ways, to recall the dreary years I passed in bondage. I would gladly forget them if I could. Yet the retrospection is not altogether without solace; for with those gloomy recollections come tender memories of my good old grandmother, like light fleecy clouds floating over a dark and troubled sea.</i>" - last paragraph of autobiography. <br /><br />"<i>Her story, as written by herself, cannot fail to interest the reader. It is a sad illustration of the condition of this country, which boasts of its civilization, while it sanctions laws and customs which make the experiences of the present more strange than any fictions of the past."</i> - <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amy_and_Isaac_Post" rel="nofollow noopener">AMY POST</a> Rochester, NY Oct 30th, 1859<br /><br />I read this book as a part of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/178461.The_Classic_Slave_Narratives" title="The Classic Slave Narratives by Henry Louis Gates Jr." rel="nofollow noopener">The Classic Slave Narratives</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5063872482348149196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2670601" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2670601" />
    <a title="Stephani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/168785-stephani"><img alt="Stephani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442898001p2/168785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2670601?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephani" href="/user/show/168785-stephani">Stephani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who wants to learn about slavery from the &quot;house negro&quot; POV</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/168785-stephani?shelf=african-diaspora">african-diaspora</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/168785-stephani?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/168785-stephani?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2670601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7297973500315309659">Next time you hear somebody going on about how the "mulatto" or "house negro" class in slave days were "privileged" and "got over" on the "field negroes," tell them to read this book. Sure, the "mulatto" or "light-skinned" slaves got to work in the house or were sometimes allowed to work away from the plantation in a trade and sometimes got to keep their own money. If they were really lucky, they might be taught to read on the sly.<br/>However, these "privileges" were likely to be taken away at any m</span>
  <span id="freeText7297973500315309659" style="display:none">Next time you hear somebody going on about how the "mulatto" or "house negro" class in slave days were "privileged" and "got over" on the "field negroes," tell them to read this book. Sure, the "mulatto" or "light-skinned" slaves got to work in the house or were sometimes allowed to work away from the plantation in a trade and sometimes got to keep their own money. If they were really lucky, they might be taught to read on the sly.<br />However, these "privileges" were likely to be taken away at any momemt at the whim of a slaveholder: If they needed money, the slave could be sold away to less comfortable circumstances. Or a "nice" slaveholer could die, leaving the slave to someone not so nice -- someone who might want to sexually abuse the slave, for instance.<br />Sexual abuse of female slaves, especially house slaves, and the sexual hypocrisy of the times really makes this book stand out from, say, Frederick Douglass' narrative. Harriet Jacobs is so worried about people judging her for turning to another White man and having babies with him in the hope that it will make her evil lech "master" leave her alone. If she had succumbed to her "master's" lechery, she would have been viewed as just another slave woman forced to be a "bed wench." However, being a woman who chooses to have sex with a man (for whatever reason) upset her grandmother and put her at risk for people shunning her! <br />This book makes you glad those days are over for more reasons than one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7297973500315309659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Charlotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11475773-charlotte"><img alt="Charlotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487528389p2/11475773.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3270639656?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte" href="/user/show/11475773-charlotte">Charlotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11475773-charlotte?shelf=black-lives-matter">black-lives-matter</a>
          </div>
